The Miracle Analogy and the Circularity Problem
Sanusi presents the miracle analogy and the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the miracle is analogous to God's confirming speech and why proving divine speech through revelation leads to circularity.

Core Idea
The miracle stands in for God's verbal confirmation, but proving God's speech from scripture is circular.

Sanusi begins by stating that a miracle functions like God saying 'You are truthful' to a prophet. But this raises a problem: if we only know God's speech through revelation, we are trapped in a circle. In this lesson, we unpack the initial analogy and the logical difficulty it introduces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the conditional structure: if… then…
2
Understand the analogy
Sanusi says the miracle's indication takes the place of a direct statement from God. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the relationship between miracles and divine speech?
The analogy assumes that a miracle can only confirm if God has speech.
3
Identify the circularity
Sanusi points out that if we try to establish God's speech through scripture (sam'), we would need that scripture to be confirmed by prophecy, which itself depends on miracles. This is circular.
This sets up the need for an alternative explanation, which Ibn al-Tilmisani provides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ دلالة المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلة قولِ اللهِ تعالى لمدعي الرسالة : صدقت ، أو : أنتَ رسولي ، فما لم يثبتِ الكلامُ الصِّدْقُ اللهِ تعالى لا يكونُ مُصدّقاً لرسله ، فلو أثبتنا الكلام له تعالى بالسمع . . لدار . قلت : قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني : ( إِنَّهُ  ُسؤال قوي ، وجوابُهُ : أَنَّ مَنِ ادَّعى أنَّهُ رسولُ الملِكِ بمرأىَ مِنَ المَلِكِ و مسمع ، وقال : آية صدقي أنْ يُغيّر الملِكُ عادته المألوفة ، ويفعل كذا ، ثم قال : أيُّها الملكُ ؛ إن كنتُ صادقاً في دعواي فافعل لي ذلكَ ، ففعل ذلك على الوجه الذي التمسه . . فيعلمُ جميعُ الحاضرينَ أَنَّهُ رسوله ، وأَنَّهُ صادقٌ ، وإِنْ كانَ فيهم مَنْ ينفي كلام النفسِ ، ويكفي في العلم بتصديقه إيجاده للفعل الدال على إرادة تصديقِهِ كما يدلُّ التخصيص في الأفعالِ على إرادةِ وقوعها على ذلك الوجه وقولهم : إنَّ المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلة التصديق بالقول.. مُسلَّم ، ولكن تتنزَّلُ منزلة المواضعة على قول يدلُّ على إرادة ذلك كما تدلُّ بعضُ الإشارات على ذلكَ ، والكلامُ المُستدَلُّ على ثبوتِهِ اللهِ تعالى بالسمع في دعوى الأشعريَّةِ هو القولُ النفسي ، والنزاع فيه ، لا في العباراتِ الحادثة المتواضع عليها ، والأفعالُ كثيراً ما تدلُّ على الإرادات وإن لم تُوضَع لذلك ، نظراً إلى العادات ، والمعجزة كذلك ((۱)
Indeed, the indication of the miracle takes the place of the statement of God Most High to the claimant of prophethood: 'You have spoken truthfully' or 'You are My messenger'. So if the truthful speech of God Most High is not established, He is not a confirmer of His messengers. For if we were to establish speech for Him Most High through hearing... it would be circular. I said: Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'It is a strong question, and its answer is: That one who claims to be the messenger of a king in the king's sight and hearing, and says: The sign of my truthfulness is that the king changes his usual practice and does such and such, then says: O king, if I am truthful in my claim, then do this for me, and he does it in the manner requested... then all those present know that he is his messenger and that he is truthful, even if among them is one who denies internal speech (kalam al-nafs). And it is sufficient for the knowledge of his confirmation that he performs the act indicating the will to confirm him, just as specification (takhsis) in actions indicates the will for them to occur in that manner. And their statement: 'The miracle takes the place of confirmation by speech' is accepted, but it takes the place of a convention upon a statement indicating the will for that, just as some indications indicate that. And the speech whose establishment for God Most High is argued by hearing in the Ash'ari claim is internal speech (al-qawl al-nafsi), and the dispute concerns it, not the originated expressions (al-'ibarat al-haditha) that are conventionally agreed upon. And actions often indicate wills even if they are not placed for that, in view of customs, and the miracle is likewise.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about how miracles indicate God's speech. It begins by stating that the miracle's indication is akin to God explicitly saying 'you are truthful' to a prophet. Then it raises a problem: if we establish God's speech only through revealed law (sam'), we would have a circular argument. Then it quotes Ibn al-Tilmisani's answer: he gives a king-messenger analogy to show that a miracle performed in response to a prophet's request can confirm his truthfulness even for those who deny internal speech. The key is that the miracle shows God's will to confirm the prophet, just as actions can indicate will even without conventional language. Then the author clarifies: the Ash'ari claim is about internal speech (qawl nafsi), not physical expressions. Actions like miracles can indicate will by custom. So the miracle analogy supports the existence of divine internal speech.

Key Terms
معجزة
تصديق
كلام
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what problem arises if we try to establish God's speech through revelation (sam')?
A) It would be impossible
B) It would be circular
C) It would require a miracle
D) It would contradict reason

Key Takeaway
The miracle's confirmation function implies divine speech, but proving that speech through revelation is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle as proof of prophethood, do you automatically accept it without considering whether God's truthfulness is already established in your mind? How would your faith be different if you could not resolve the circularity Sanusi points out?
?
Can you identify any of your own beliefs about God that you have accepted without examining their logical foundations? This passage shows how easy it is to fall into circular reasoning—are there hidden circles in your own arguments for faith?
?
If the miracle alone is not sufficient without the prior knowledge of God's speech, how confident are you in the proofs you use to defend Islam? Would you be willing to re-examine them honestly, even if it leads to discomfort?

Ibn al-Tilmisani's Resolution: Miracles Indicate Will
Sanusi presents Ibn al-Tilmisani's answer to the circularity objection using the king analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Tilmisani's king-messenger analogy and how it shows that a miracle indicates God's will to confirm, avoiding circularity.

Core Idea
A miracle confirms a prophet by indicating God's will, like a king's action indicates his will without explicit speech.

In response to the circularity problem, Ibn al-Tilmisani offers a powerful analogy: a king changes his custom in response to a claimant, showing his will to confirm. Similarly, a miracle shows God's will to confirm the prophet, even for those who deny internal speech. This lesson examines that analogy and the distinction between internal speech and conventional expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage, then the translation, paying attention to the king analogy and the subsequent clarification.
The passage begins with 'قلت' indicating an answer to a previous objection.
2
Analyze the king analogy
Ibn al-Tilmisani describes a claimant before a king who asks for a sign: the king changes his custom and performs an act. The act, not a verbal statement, confirms the claimant's truth. How does this mirror a miracle?
Notice that the act indicates the king's will to confirm, even without a direct statement.
3
Understand how actions indicate will
The passage states that actions often indicate wills by custom, not by convention. Miracles similarly indicate God's will to confirm the prophet.
This avoids the need for a direct divine statement, resolving circularity.
4
Grasp the clarification about internal speech
Sanusi distinguishes between internal speech (qawl nafsi) and conventional expressions. The Ash'ari claim about divine speech concerns internal speech, not physical sounds.
This clarifies that the miracle indicates will, not necessarily speech, but aligns with the Ash'ari position on internal speech.

فإنَّ دلالة المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلة قولِ اللهِ تعالى لمدعي الرسالة : صدقت ، أو : أنتَ رسولي ، فما لم يثبتِ الكلامُ الصِّدْقُ اللهِ تعالى لا يكونُ مُصدّقاً لرسله ، فلو أثبتنا الكلام له تعالى بالسمع . . لدار . قلت : قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني : ( إِنَّهُ  ُسؤال قوي ، وجوابُهُ : أَنَّ مَنِ ادَّعى أنَّهُ رسولُ الملِكِ بمرأىَ مِنَ المَلِكِ و مسمع ، وقال : آية صدقي أنْ يُغيّر الملِكُ عادته المألوفة ، ويفعل كذا ، ثم قال : أيُّها الملكُ ؛ إن كنتُ صادقاً في دعواي فافعل لي ذلكَ ، ففعل ذلك على الوجه الذي التمسه . . فيعلمُ جميعُ الحاضرينَ أَنَّهُ رسوله ، وأَنَّهُ صادقٌ ، وإِنْ كانَ فيهم مَنْ ينفي كلام النفسِ ، ويكفي في العلم بتصديقه إيجاده للفعل الدال على إرادة تصديقِهِ كما يدلُّ التخصيص في الأفعالِ على إرادةِ وقوعها على ذلك الوجه وقولهم : إنَّ المعجزة تتنزَّلُ منزلة التصديق بالقول.. مُسلَّم ، ولكن تتنزَّلُ منزلة المواضعة على قول يدلُّ على إرادة ذلك كما تدلُّ بعضُ الإشارات على ذلكَ ، والكلامُ المُستدَلُّ على ثبوتِهِ اللهِ تعالى بالسمع في دعوى الأشعريَّةِ هو القولُ النفسي ، والنزاع فيه ، لا في العباراتِ الحادثة المتواضع عليها ، والأفعالُ كثيراً ما تدلُّ على الإرادات وإن لم تُوضَع لذلك ، نظراً إلى العادات ، والمعجزة كذلك ((۱)
Indeed, the indication of the miracle takes the place of the statement of God Most High to the claimant of prophethood: 'You have spoken truthfully' or 'You are My messenger'. So if the truthful speech of God Most High is not established, He is not a confirmer of His messengers. For if we were to establish speech for Him Most High through hearing... it would be circular. I said: Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'It is a strong question, and its answer is: That one who claims to be the messenger of a king in the king's sight and hearing, and says: The sign of my truthfulness is that the king changes his usual practice and does such and such, then says: O king, if I am truthful in my claim, then do this for me, and he does it in the manner requested... then all those present know that he is his messenger and that he is truthful, even if among them is one who denies internal speech (kalam al-nafs). And it is sufficient for the knowledge of his confirmation that he performs the act indicating the will to confirm him, just as specification (takhsis) in actions indicates the will for them to occur in that manner. And their statement: 'The miracle takes the place of confirmation by speech' is accepted, but it takes the place of a convention upon a statement indicating the will for that, just as some indications indicate that. And the speech whose establishment for God Most High is argued by hearing in the Ash'ari claim is internal speech (al-qawl al-nafsi), and the dispute concerns it, not the originated expressions (al-'ibarat al-haditha) that are conventionally agreed upon. And actions often indicate wills even if they are not placed for that, in view of customs, and the miracle is likewise.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about how miracles indicate God's speech. It begins by stating that the miracle's indication is akin to God explicitly saying 'you are truthful' to a prophet. Then it raises a problem: if we establish God's speech only through revealed law (sam'), we would have a circular argument. Then it quotes Ibn al-Tilmisani's answer: he gives a king-messenger analogy to show that a miracle performed in response to a prophet's request can confirm his truthfulness even for those who deny internal speech. The key is that the miracle shows God's will to confirm the prophet, just as actions can indicate will even without conventional language. Then the author clarifies: the Ash'ari claim is about internal speech (qawl nafsi), not physical expressions. Actions like miracles can indicate will by custom. So the miracle analogy supports the existence of divine internal speech.

Key Terms
كلام النفس
إرادة
عادة
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a miracle must be a direct divine statement or that it proves prophethood only if God explicitly speaks.
Correction: The passage explains that a miracle indicates God's will to confirm, not a direct statement. Even without asserting internal speech, the act itself suffices to confirm truthfulness.

Checkpoint
In Ibn al-Tilmisani's analogy, what does the king's action indicate?
A) The king's internal speech
B) The king's will to confirm the claimant
C) The claimant's honesty
D) A pre-arranged signal

Key Takeaway
Miracles confirm prophethood by indicating God's will to confirm, thus avoiding the circularity of proving divine speech through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the story of the king and the claimant, do you believe you would have been convinced? Or would you have demanded explicit words from the king himself—and what does that say about your demand for signs from God? Are you willing to accept God's will made manifest in creation without demanding a verbal thunderbolt?
?
This lesson teaches that a miracle is not a divine speech act but an indication of will. How does this change the way you approach the miracles of the Quran or the splitting of the moon? Are you looking for a message in the act itself, or for the will behind it? Commit today to seeking God's will in every sign, not just the spectacular ones.
?
Where in your life do you demand explicit divine speech rather than trusting the indications of His will? What area of your life are you holding out for a direct command—and how can you start reading His will through His actions in your life?

Abu Ishaq's Proof from Kingship for God's Speech
Sanusi presents Abu Ishaq's argument from kingship for God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's argument that God must possess the attribute of speech because He is a king and kingship requires command and prohibition.

Core Idea
God's kingship demands speech as a distinct eternal attribute.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the proof of Abu Ishaq al-Asfara'ini for God's attribute of speech. The argument begins with a simple observation: God is a king, and a king's rule is realized through issuing commands and prohibitions. Since creatures are in need of such directives, God must possess speech, the attribute that enables command and prohibition. Abu Ishaq then deepens the argument by showing that this contingent attribute must be rooted in an eternal attribute of God, and it cannot be reduced to will or knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Notice that the argument starts with the premise that God is a king (malik). This is taken as given from revelation or reason.
2
Follow the kingship logic
Kingship is incomplete without command and prohibition. Reflect on the nature of authority and governance.
3
Understand the contingent-eternal principle
Any attribute that is possible (ja'iz) for God must be based on an eternal attribute. This is a key principle in kalam.
4
Eliminate alternative explanations
Command and prohibition cannot be reduced to will or knowledge; they require speech. See why that is.
5
Conclude
Therefore, God must have eternal speech (kalam nafsi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتج الأستاذ أبو إسحاق على أنَّهُ تعالى مُتكلّم : بأنه سبحانَهُ مَلِكٌ ، ولا يتم الملك إلا بأمرٍ ونهي، ولجواز تردُّدِ الخلائق بين أمر مطاع ونهي مُتَّبع (۱) ، وقال : كلُّ صفةٍ جائزة لا بد وأن تستند إلى صفةٍ أزليَّةِ ، وإلا استحالَ ما عُلِمَ جوازُهُ ، ويستحيلُ ردُّ الأمر والنهي إلى الإرادة أو العلم وسائر الصفاتِ غيرِ الكلام النفسي على ما سنبينه عند إثباتِ صفة الكلام في فصلِ إثباتِ صفاتِ المعاني (٢) ، فيجب إثباتُهُ للهِ تعالى . والطريقة الأولى تؤول إلى نفي النقائص ، وقد عرفت ما في الاستناد في نفيها إلى العقل .
And the master Abu Ishaq argued that He Most High is Mutakallim (speaking): because He, glory to Him, is a king (malik), and kingship is only complete with command (amr) and prohibition (nahy), and because of the possibility of creatures being in a state of needing an obeyed command and followed prohibition. And he said: Every contingent attribute (sifa ja'iza) must be based on an eternal attribute, otherwise what is known to be possible would be impossible. And it is impossible to refer command and prohibition to will (irada) or knowledge ('ilm) and the rest of attributes other than internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), as we will clarify when establishing the attribute of speech in the chapter on establishing attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). So its establishment for God Most High is obligatory. And the first method (al-tariqa al-ula) leads to negation of imperfections (nafy al-naqa'is), and you have known what there is in basing the negation of them on reason.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Ishaq's proof that God is Mutakallim (possessing speech). The argument runs: God is a king, and kingship is only complete through issuing commands and prohibitions. Furthermore, creatures are in need of an authoritative command and prohibition. Therefore, God must have speech (the attribute of command and prohibition). He adds that any contingent attribute (like command/prohibition) must be rooted in an eternal attribute; otherwise it would be impossible for it to occur. He then argues that command and prohibition cannot be reduced to will or knowledge; they require speech. So speech must be an eternal attribute of God. The author then comments that the first method (likely the miracle method) leads to denying imperfections, but basing that on reason has issues.

Key Terms
مَلِك
أمر
نهي
صفة جائزة
صفة أزلية
كلام نفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command and prohibition are just expressions of God's will.
Correction: They require the attribute of speech because will alone does not specify directive content; speech is the attribute that conveys commands and prohibitions.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq, why must God have speech?
A) Because He is all-powerful
B) Because He is a king and kingship requires command and prohibition
C) Because the Quran is His speech
D) Because He revealed His will

Key Takeaway
God's kingship necessitates the eternal attribute of internal speech to issue commands and prohibitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear God's command to pray, do you truly receive it as a direct utterance from the King of the universe, or do you treat it as a mere recommendation? What would change in your consistency if you believed every command is the speech of the King?
?
Abu Ishaq argues that kingship is incomplete without command and prohibition. Where in your life have you neglected God's commands or prohibitions, effectively denying His complete kingship over you? What is one specific command you will start obeying today as an act of acknowledging His sovereignty?
?
The proof insists that command cannot be reduced to will. Are there commands of God that you have dismissed as 'just what God wants' rather than as authoritative speech? How would your heart respond differently if you heard each command as a direct address from the King? Commit to reading one passage of the Qur'an this week as God's eternal speech to you, not just a text.

The Impossibility of Change in the Eternal Essence and Attributes
Sanusi proves the absolute immutability of the eternal essence and attributes and lists its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal essence and its attributes cannot undergo any change, and how this principle guards against misconceptions about divine attributes.

Core Idea
The eternal essence and its attributes are absolutely immutable; any change would imply non-existence.

Sanusi now builds on the established necessity of the divine attributes' eternity to prove their absolute immutability. He explains that just as non-existence is impossible for the eternal, so is change. This principle then yields specific consequences for each attribute, ruling out any imperfection or dependency.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the universal principle
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states that whatever is necessarily eternal cannot cease to exist. This is the foundation for the rest of the argument.
The principle is repeated throughout the passage.
2
Apply to essence and attributes
Notice that the same reasoning applies to both the eternal essence and its attributes. Change in either would violate their necessary eternity.
3
Examine the specific impossibilities
Read the list of what is impossible for each attribute: knowledge cannot be acquired or have forgetfulness, power cannot need tools, etc. Ask yourself why each would entail change.
The text explicitly says all these entail change and temporality.
4
Understand the detailed proof
Study the final part where Sanusi explains that change from non-existence to existence is prevented by eternity, and from existence to non-existence by subsistence.
This is a condensed demonstration using the definitions of eternity and subsistence.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching guards against anthropomorphic conceptions of God. The absolute immutability ensures that God is not subject to any imperfection or temporal change.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصح إذاً : أنَّ الفعل يدلُّ على وجوب تلك الصفاتِ وجوباً مطلقاً بحسَبِ الذاتِ ، وذكرُ الدورِ والتسلسل في هذا الجواب بيان لوجهِ دلالتِهِ على ذلك ، والله الموفق . وقوله : ( لتوقف إحداثها عليها ) أي : على أمثالها . ما وجب قدمه استحال عدمُهُ . وإذا عرفتَ وجوب قدم الصفاتِ.. عرفت استحالة عدمها ؛ لما قدَّمنا مِنْ بيانِ استحالة العدم على القديم هذا ظاهر ، وقد قدمنا برهان القاعدة الكليَّةِ ؛ وهي أنَّ كلَّ ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه . ذات القديم وصفاته لا تقبل التغير مطلقاً . فخرج لك بهذا استحالة التغير على القديم مطلقاً . أمَّا في ذاتِهِ : فلوجوب قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ؛ لما مرَّ . وأما في صفاتِهِ : فلما ذُكِرَ الآنَ . ومِنْ ثَمَّ استحال على علمِهِ تعالى أنْ يكونَ كَسْبيّاً ؛ أي : يحصلُ لهُ دليل ، أو ضرورياً ؛ أي : يقارنه ضرر ؛ كعلمنا بألمنا أو يطرأ عليه سهو أو غفلةٌ ، واستحال على قدرتِهِ أنْ تحتاج إلى آلةٍ أو معاونة ، وعلى إرادته أن تكون لغرض ، وعلى سمعه وبصرِهِ وكلامِهِ وإدراكه على القول بهِ أنْ تكون بجارحة أو مقابلة أو اتصال ، أو يكونَ كلامه حرفاً أو صوتاً ، أو يطرأ عليهِ سكوتُ ؛ لاستلزام جميع ما ذُكِرَ التغير والحدوث . هذا كله ظاهر ، ولنزده بياناً فنقول : أما وجه استحالةِ التغير على الذاتِ العليَّةِ وعلى صفاتها : فلأنَّهُ إِنْ كانَ مِنْ عدم إلى وجود .. فوجوبُ القدم للذات الكريمة ولجميعِ صفاتها يمنع ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن سلبِ العدم السابق على الوجودِ ، وإنْ كانَ مِنْ وجودِ إلى عدم .. فوجوبُ البقاء لهما يدفعُهُ ؛ لأنَّه عبارةٌ عن سلبِ العدم اللاحق بعد الوجودِ ، وقد سبق في العقيدة ذكرُ برهانِ وجوب القدم والبقاء للذات العليَّةِ ولصفاتها
So it is established that the act indicates the necessity of those attributes absolutely according to the essence, and the mention of circularity and regress in this answer is an explanation of the aspect of its indication of that, and God is the Grantor of success. And his saying: (because their origination depends on them) i.e., on their likes. Whatever is necessarily eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And if you know the necessity of the eternity of the attributes, you know the impossibility of their non-existence, due to what we have previously presented of the demonstration of the impossibility of non-existence upon the eternal. This is apparent, and we have previously presented the proof of the universal principle, which is that everything whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible. The essence of the Eternal and His attributes absolutely do not accept change. So there emerges for you from this the impossibility of change upon the Eternal absolutely. As for His essence: due to the necessity of its eternity and subsistence, as has passed. And as for His attributes: due to what was mentioned now. And therefore it is impossible for His knowledge—exalted is He—to be acquired, i.e., that a proof obtains for it, or necessary, i.e., that harm accompanies it, like our knowledge of our pain, or that forgetfulness or inattention occur to it; and it is impossible for His power to need an instrument or assistance; and impossible for His will to be for a purpose; and impossible for His hearing, sight, speech, and perception according to the view affirming it to be through a limb, facing, or connection, or that His speech be a letter or sound, or that silence occur to Him, because all that is mentioned entails change and temporality. All this is apparent, and let us increase its clarification, so we say: As for the aspect of the impossibility of change upon the exalted essence and upon His attributes: because if it is from non-existence to existence, then the necessity of eternity for the noble essence and for all His attributes prevents that, for it is the negation of the non-existence preceding existence; and if it is from existence to non-existence, then the necessity of subsistence for both repels it, for it is the negation of the non-existence subsequent to existence; and the proof of the necessity of eternity and subsistence for the exalted essence and for His attributes has already preceded in the creed.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the absolute immutability of the eternal divine essence and attributes. The author, after a preceding argument, establishes that the act (i.e., the existence of contingent beings) indicates the absolute necessity of the divine attributes with respect to the essence. He then notes that the mention of circularity and regress in the answer clarifies how the act indicates that necessity. He then states a general principle: whatever is necessarily eternal, its non-existence is impossible. From this, knowing that the attributes are necessarily eternal, one knows that their non-existence is impossible, because that is a universal rule. Then he asserts that the essence of the Eternal and His attributes absolutely do not accept change. This conclusion is then applied: change is impossible for the essence because of the necessity of its eternity and subsistence, and for the attributes because of what was just mentioned (their necessity and eternity). From this, he lists specific impossibilities for each attribute: divine knowledge cannot be acquired (dependent on a proof) or necessary (like pain), nor can it have forgetfulness; divine power cannot need instruments or assistance; divine will cannot be for a purpose; hearing, sight, speech, and perception cannot be via limbs, facing, connection, or letters/sounds; and speech cannot have silence. All these would imply change and temporality. After stating that this is clear, he provides a more detailed demonstration of why change is impossible: if the supposed change were from non-existence to existence, the necessity of eternity prevents it (eternity means negation of prior non-existence); if from existence to non-existence, the necessity of subsistence repels it (subsistence means negation of subsequent non-existence). He references that the proof of the necessity of eternity and subsistence for the essence and attributes has already been given in the creed. The argument thus proceeds from the established necessity of eternity and subsistence to the absolute negation of any change.

Key Terms
وجوب القدم
استحالة العدم
التغير
الذات العليَّة
الصفات
وجوب البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only the essence is immutable, while attributes can change or be acquired.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly includes both the essence and attributes, explaining that the same reasoning applies to both.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is it impossible for divine knowledge to be acquired (kashf) or necessary (daruri)?
A) Because knowledge is eternal and thus cannot depend on causes.
B) Because acquired and necessary knowledge imply change and temporality.
C) Because God does not need knowledge.
D) Because such knowledge is only for humans.

Key Takeaway
The eternal essence and all its attributes are absolutely immutable, and any conception implying change or temporality is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that every attribute of God is eternally fixed—His knowledge, power, will, and justice never change. Yet, when you sin, do you secretly hope that God's justice might be 'overlooked' or that His anger will cool over time? What would change in your repentance if you truly believed that God's knowledge of every sin you have committed is eternally present to Him, and His justice is as unchanging as His mercy? Write down one sin you need to return to God about, not expecting leniency but trusting the certainty of His forgiveness through repentance, because even His mercy is unchanging.
?
The passage says God's will cannot be for a purpose—He does not act out of need. How often do you worship God with the hidden condition that He must give you something in return, like health or success? Can you commit to one act of worship this week—a prayer, a fast, a charity—that you do with absolutely no expectation of personal benefit, simply because God's command is unchanging and He alone is worthy?
?
If God's knowledge is not acquired and never forgets, then every moment of your life—every thought, word, and deed—is fully present to Him at every instant. When you think of a past sin you have already repented from, do you assume God has 'moved on' and that He no longer sees it? How does the reality of God's unchanging knowledge affect your understanding of His forgiveness—does it make you more grateful, more careful, or more complacent? Resolve to thank God daily for His perfect knowledge that sees your weakness and still forgives.

What Proofs Establish About Allah
Sanusi enumerates definitive attributes of Allah established by proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitive attributes of Allah that are established by rational and scriptural proofs, and the testimony of proofs to these truths.

Core Idea
Allah is a self-subsistent essence, independent, unlike creation, and all else depends on Him.

Sanusi summarizes the core findings of Islamic theology: what can be definitively known about Allah through reason and revelation. This passage lists the essential attributes that define Allah's transcendence and perfection, establishing a foundation for all subsequent theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify attributes
Read the passage carefully and list the attributes and negations mentioned about Allah.
2
Notice the structure
The passage begins with 'self-subsistent essence' and expands with negations and positive attributes.
3
Reflect on 'not an attribute, not a body'
Consider why Sanusi explicitly negates that Allah is an attribute or a body.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Understand the evidence
Note that the passage ends by stating that rational proofs and transmitted revelation agree on these truths.

وبالجملة : فالمقطوع به بشهادة البراهين العقلية والقواطع السمعيَّةِ : أَنَّهُ  َجلَّ وعلا ذات قائم بنفسِهِ ؛ أي : مستغن عن المحل والمؤثر ؛ لوجوب وجودِهِ ، موصوف بما لا يُحاطُ بِهِ مِنْ صفاتِ الجلال والجمال ، ليسَ بصفةٍ مِنَ الصفاتِ ، ولا جرماً تجري عليهِ الحوادث والتغيراتُ ، ولا تمرُّ عليه الأزمنة ولا يتخصَّص بالجهات . لا يقبلُ اجتماعاً ولا افتراقاً ، ولا صِغَراً ولا كِبَراً ، لا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير ، ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير ، كلُّ الممكناتِ مُفتقرةٌ إِليه ؛ وهو الغني عن جميعها في الأزلِ وفيما لا يزالُ ، وهو على كلِّ شيءٍ قدير . كلُّ ذلك شهدَتْ بهِ البراهين المنتهية إلى ضرورات العقول وطابق فيها المعقول المنقولُ ، ثم عَجَزَتِ العقولُ بعد عن الإدراكِ ، وانقطع تشرُّفُها للخوض فيما خرج عن دائرة التوهمات والتخيلات وقُصارى أمرِها : أنَّها صارَتْ مِنْ أجلِ اللمحة التي لَحَظَتْ ، والرَّمزةِ التي بها غابَتْ عن العوالم كلها وفيها تاهَتْ وبها وَلِهَتْ.. تتطايرُ مِنْ وراءِ حُجُبِ الكبرياء وأردية العزّ ؛ شوقاً إلى ما لا يُكيَّفُ مِنْ جميل اللقاء ، وتتنسَّمُ مِنْ مواهب الزيادةِ لكشف الغطاء ، ما تُروِّحُ بهِ على القلب المحترق الأحشاء ، وربَّما عظُمَ الشوقُ بلطْفِ نسيم
And in summary: what is definitely established by the testimony of rational proofs (al-barāhīn al-ʿaqliyya) and decisive scriptural evidences (al-qawāṭiʿ al-samʿiyya) is that He, exalted and high, is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt qāʾim bi-nafsihi); i.e., independent of substrate and effecter because of the necessity of His existence, described with what cannot be encompassed of the attributes of majesty and beauty, not an attribute among attributes, nor a body on which events and changes occur, nor do times pass over Him, nor is He specified by directions. He does not accept union nor separation, nor smallness nor greatness, no likeness for Him nor peer, no opponent nor vizier. All possible things are in need of Him; and He is independent of all of them in pre-eternity and in what never ceases, and He is over all things powerful. All of that is testified to by proofs ending in necessities of intellects, and the transmitted (al-manqūl) is in accordance with the rational therein. Then intellects became incapable after that of comprehension, and their honor in delving into what lies outside the circle of imaginings and representations was cut off. The utmost of their affair is that they became, because of the glimpse they caught and the glance by which they were hidden from all worlds, and in it they became lost and infatuated... they flutter from behind the veils of majesty and the robes of might, longing for what cannot be qualified of beautiful encounter, and they catch a breeze from the gifts of increase for the unveiling of the covering, what gives relief to the heart burning with longing, and perhaps the longing increases by the gentle breeze.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding summary of what is definitively established about God through rational arguments (barāhīn ʿaqliyya) and decisive scriptural proofs (qawāṭiʿ samʿiyya). It is a summation passage. The author lists key attributes: God is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt qāʾim bi-nafsihi), meaning He does not need a place (maḥall) or an effecter (muʾaththir), due to the necessity of His existence (wujūb wujūd). He is described with majestic and beautiful attributes that cannot be fully grasped. He is not an attribute itself (which would need a substrate), nor a body subject to change, time, or direction. He does not accept union, separation, smallness, or greatness. He has no likeness, peer, opponent, or vizier. All contingent beings (mumkināt) depend on Him, while He is eternally independent. He is omnipotent. The author then notes that these truths are evidenced by proofs that lead to intellectual necessities, and that transmitted revelation (al-manqūl) agrees with reason (al-maʿqūl). However, beyond this rational and scriptural knowledge, intellects fall short of further comprehension; they cannot delve into what lies beyond imagination and conceptualization. At best, they achieve a glimpse that makes them forget worlds, and they yearn for a vision of the Unqualifiable. The passage ends with a mystical description of the soul's longing for God.

القواطع السمعية
ذات قائم بنفسه
الممكنات
الغني
المعقول
المنقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God is an attribute, a body, or has parts and direction.
Correction: The text explicitly negates these: ليس بصفة ولا جرما ولا يتخصص بالجهات ولا يقبل اجتماعا ولا افتراقا.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an attribute of Allah?
A) Self-subsistent
B) Subject to change
C) Independent of all things
D) Omnipotent

Key Takeaway
Allah is definitively known as a self-subsistent, transcendent Being, utterly unlike creation, and all creation depends on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that Allah is not like anything—yet your heart forms images when you think of Him. What specific mental image do you hold that contradicts this truth, and what will you do to erase it today?
?
If every possible being—including you—depends entirely on Allah, where in your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient? Will you ask Allah to make you feel your need for Him in that area before you sleep tonight?
?
The passage says Allah is independent of all creation. When your prayers seem unanswered, do you secretly resent Allah as if He owes you? How will you repent from that assumption right now?

The Limits of Intellect and the Soul's Longing
Sanusi describes the incapacity of intellects and the soul's longing for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the limitations of human reason after establishing basic truths about God, and the mystical yearning for a vision of the Unqualifiable.

Core Idea
After reason affirms God's existence and attributes, it must recognize its incapacity to comprehend His essence.

Having summarized what can be definitively known about Allah, Sanusi turns to the limits of human intellect. He acknowledges that reason and revelation agree on these truths, but then emphasizes that beyond this, intellect fails utterly. The passage transitions into a mystical description of the soul's longing for God, highlighting the harmony between rational theology and spiritual yearning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part
Starting from 'ثم عجزت العقول', read the passage and note the shift in tone and content.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where Sanusi states that intellects become incapable after comprehending basic truths.
3
Observe the poetic language
Notice words like 'تتطاير', 'شوقاً', 'ولِهَت' – what do they describe?
This reflects the soul's yearning.
4
Reflect on 'ما لا يكيَّف'
Consider what it means that the beautiful encounter (اللقاء) cannot be qualified or described.

Proof of the Oneness of the Creator
Sanusi presents a concise proof that the Creator must be one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's concise proof that the Creator must be one, including its reliance on the impossibility of two necessary beings with potential conflict.

Core Idea
The existence of two creators leads to logical absurdities.

Sanusi now moves from establishing the existence of a maker to proving that this maker must be one. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum assuming a second deity and showing that it would entail incapacity or subjugation, both impossible for a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof text carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if there were a second, then...'
The text is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the claim and the two scenarios
Sanusi first states the claim: the Maker must be one. Then he presents two possibilities if there were a second: either they differ, or they necessarily agree.
3
Understand the consequence of difference
If they differ, both or one becomes incapable. Why? Because each would want opposite things, but as independent necessary beings, none can be thwarted—so difference implies incapacity.
This is absurd for a necessary being.
4
Understand the consequence of necessary agreement
If they necessarily agree, then one is subjugated (qahr) by the other, forced to agree. But a necessary being cannot be coerced.
Also impossible.
5
See how the proof eliminates a second
The argument also notes that if agreement is not necessary (i.e., difference possible), incapacity returns. Thus all possibilities fail, proving oneness.
The footnote illustrates the logic with Zayd's stillness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير دليل الوحدانية ]
ثم نقولُ : يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً ؛ إذ لو كانَ مَعَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزُهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلافِ ، وقهرهما أو قهرُ أحدهما عند الاتفاق الواجب ، مع استحالة ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ لكلّ واحدٍ باعتبار الانفراد (۱) ، ونفي وجوب الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما عن كلِّ منهما ، فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ
قبولُهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ .
(۱) لأنه إذا توجّه أحدهما إلى تسكين زيد مثلاً ، والفرض : أن الآخر يجب عليه موافقة صاحبه .. كان التحريك في جانبه مستحيلاً ، وقد كان ممكناً في حقه قبل أن يتوجه صاحبه إلى التسكين . ( حامدي » ( ص۳۰۸ ) .
Presentation of the proof of oneness. Then we say: It is necessary for this Maker to be one; for if there were with Him a second, then the incapacity of both or the incapacity of one of them would follow in the case of difference, and the subjugation of both or the subjugation of one of them in the case of necessary agreement, along with the impossibility of what was known to be possible for each one in terms of independence, and the negation of the necessity of existence for each one of them, because each one is free of need for the other. For if their agreement is not necessary but rather their difference is permissible, then the acceptance of incapacity would follow, and the first [point] returns. (1) Because if one of them intends to still Zayd, for example, and the supposition is that the other must agree with the first, then movement on the part of the second would be impossible, despite having been possible for him before the first intended stillness.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof of the oneness of the Creator (tawhid). The author begins by stating the claim: this Maker (al-sāniʿ) must be one. The proof is a conditional argument assuming a second deity. If there were a second, two cases arise: either they differ or they agree necessarily. If they differ, both would be incapable (because each would be unable to carry out its will) or at least one would be incapable. If they agree necessarily, then one is subjugated (qahr) by the other in the sense of being forced to conform. Both possibilities are impossible for a necessary being, who must be self-sufficient and not subject to incapacity or subjugation. The proof also notes that if agreement is not necessary (i.e., difference is possible), then incapacity again follows because each could be stymied by the other. The footnote illustrates: if one wants to still Zayd and the other must agree, the second cannot move—yet movement was possible for him alone. Thus, the impossibility of a second is established.

Key Terms
وحدانية
عجز
اختلاف
اتفاق
صانع
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of two gods from the start.
Correction: No, it is a reductio ad absurdum: it starts from a premise already established (the existence of a Maker) and shows that assuming a second leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what follows if two gods differ?
A) Both must agree.
B) Both or one become incapable.
C) One subjugates the other.
D) They cooperate perfectly.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of the Creator is rationally necessary because multiple creators would lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the oneness of Allah is rationally necessary, why do you sometimes act as if there are multiple sources of control in your life? Where do you turn to when you feel powerless or anxious about circumstances?
?
The proof shows that a second deity would either be incapable or subjugated. How does this truth confront your moments of doubt about Allah's absolute sovereignty over your failures, successes, and future?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do your fears and anxieties reflect a heart that trusts in His sole power? What would change in your daily decisions if you truly internalized that no force can override His will?

The Three Demands of Tawhid
Sanusi enumerates and explains the three demands of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and meaning of the three demands (matalib) that define the study of tawhid.

Core Idea
The study of tawhid is structured around three foundational demands.

Sanusi begins this chapter by laying out three essential demands that organize the rational investigation of God's oneness. Before diving into proofs, he clarifies what each demand aims to establish: the unity of essence, the uniqueness of God in divinity and creation, and the total dissimilarity of God from all created things. This passage gives you the blueprint for the entire discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of demands
Carefully read the Arabic passage listing the three demands. Notice the phrasing and how each demand builds on the previous.
The first demand is about the essence itself (no composition).
2
Understand each demand's meaning
For each demand, identify what it negates or affirms about God. Use the translation to clarify the precise theological point.
3
Note the cross-reference
Sanusi mentions that the first demand's proof was already given earlier. Understand that the focus of this chapter is on the second and third.
This connects the new material to prior learning.
4
Reflect on the overall structure
Consider why these three demands are sufficient to cover the concept of tawhid. How do they relate to the attributes of God?
This sets the stage for the proof in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ الكلام في هذا الفصل مُرتّب على ثلاثة مطالب : الأول : إقامة البرهان على وحدة الذاتِ ؛ بمعنى : نفي تركيبها وعدم انقسامها .
الثاني : نفي نظير له تعالى أو قسيم في الألوهية ، وفي انفراده تعالى بإيجادِ جميع الكائناتِ ذواتاً كانَتْ أو أفعالاً إسنادِ التأثيرِ لغيرِهِ في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ : معناه وعدم
الثالثُ : وَحدتُه تعالى ؛ بمعنى : مخالفته لجميع الحوادثِ ، فلا مثل له منها ، كما أنَّهُ لا ضدَّ له فيها
أما المطلب الأوّلُ : فقد سبق الكلامُ عليهِ عندَ ذكر تنزُّهِهِ تعالى عن الجرمية والتركيب ، فانظره هنالِكَ (۱)
وأمَّا برهانُ المطلب الثاني : فهو الذي تعرض له هنا ، فنقولُ : الدليل على نفْي شريك لهُ تعالى في أُلوهِيَّتِهِ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ مَعَهُ إِلهُ آخِرُ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يختلفا في الإرادة على حكم التضاد أو يتفقا ، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّم مثله أما الملازمةُ : فدليلُها ما سبقَ مِنْ وجوب عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله : وقدرته وسائرِ صفاتِهِ المتعلّقة ، فلو كانَ ثَمَّ إلهانِ لَوجبَ تعلُّقُ إرادةِ كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ بكلّ ممكن ، ومهما تعلّق بالفعل إرادتان لم يخلُ مِنَ الاتفاق عليه أو التباين
وأما بطلان التالي : فببطلان طرفيهِ ؛ وهما الاختلافُ والاتفاق
(۱) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ )
Know that the discussion in this chapter is arranged into three demands: the first: establishing the proof of the unity of the essence, meaning the negation of its composition and its indivisibility. The second: the negation of a peer or counterpart for Him, exalted, in divinity, and in His, exalted, being unique in bringing into existence all beings, whether substances or actions, and the non-attribution of effect to other than Him in anything among possible things: its meaning and [the negation of]... The third: His, exalted, unity, meaning His dissimilarity to all originated things, so there is no likeness to Him from them, just as He has no opposite in them. As for the first demand, the discussion on it has already preceded when mentioning His, exalted, transcendence from corporeality and composition, so look there. (1) As for the proof of the second demand, it is what is presented here. So we say: The evidence for the negation of a partner for Him, exalted, in His divinity is that if there were another god with Him, it would be free from [two possibilities]: either they would differ in will on the basis of contradiction, or they would agree. The consequent, in its two divisions, is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the entailment, its evidence is what has preceded regarding the necessity of the universal relation of the will of God and His power and the rest of His related attributes. For if there were two gods, then the will of each one of them and His power must relate to every possible thing. And whenever two wills relate to an act, they are not free from agreement upon it or divergence. And as for the invalidity of the consequent, it is by the invalidity of its two sides, which are divergence and agreement. (1) It has preceded (p. 248).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a structured presentation of three demands (maṭālib) concerning tawhid (divine oneness). The author first lists them: (1) proof of the unity of the divine essence, i.e., that it is not composed or divisible; (2) negation of any peer or counterpart in divinity, meaning God alone creates and nothing else has real causal influence; (3) God's unity in the sense of being unlike originated things—no likeness, no opposite. The author then notes that the first demand was already covered earlier in the book (at the discussion of transcendence from corporeality). The focus here is the second demand's proof. The proof is a standard kalam argument: if there were a second god, they must either differ or agree in will. Both are impossible: if they differ, one would be thwarted, implying incapacity; if they agree, one is unnecessary or subjugated. The impossibility of both outcomes proves that a second god cannot exist. The author also provides the logical connection (mulāzama): each god's will and power must relate to every possible, so if two wills relate to the same act, they can only agree or differ; both are impossible for a necessary being. Thus, the proof is complete.

وحدة الذات
نظير
قسيم

شريك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the three demands are separate and unrelated proofs.
Correction: The three demands are interrelated aspects of tawhid, all stemming from the same rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the first demand of tawhid establish?
A) God's unity in action
B) Unity of essence (no composition)
C) God's dissimilarity to creation
D) Negation of a partner

Key Takeaway
The three demands of tawhid are: unity of essence, negation of a peer, and dissimilarity to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second demand negates any peer to Allah in divinity. Think about your deepest fears, hopes, and reliance. Is there any person, status, or possession that you secretly treat as a 'peer' to Allah? Write down one such attachment and commit to uprooting it through tawakkul.
?
When you picture Allah, do you ever imagine Him with human-like qualities? The third demand says He is utterly dissimilar to all originated things. What specific mental image of Allah do you need to abandon? Pledge to replace it with the names and attributes revealed in the Qur’an and Sunnah.
?
The first demand teaches that Allah's essence is not composed. How does knowing that Allah is not like any created thing change the way you approach Him in your prayers? Will you pause before each prayer to remind yourself of His absolute oneness and transcendence? Decide on one concrete reminder to do this today.

Proof Against a Second Deity
Sanusi provides a logical proof for the negation of a partner in divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument (burhan) that proves the impossibility of a second deity.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, they would disagree or agree; both are impossible.

Having laid out the three demands, Sanusi now delivers the proof for the second demand: the impossibility of a second god. This is a classic kalam argument that uses the attributes of will and power. By following the logical entailment, you will see why the existence of two gods is rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage that begins 'وأمَّا برهانُ المطلب الثاني'. Pause and try to follow the logic.
The proof is a conditional argument (if...then).
2
Identify the possibilities
The argument presents two possibilities: the two gods either differ in will or agree. Understand why these are the only options.
3
Understand the entailment
The author explains why two wills must relate to each possible thing, leading to the disjunction.
This relies on the attributes of will and power.
4
Grasp the impossibility of both sides
Why is disagreement impossible? Because it would imply incapacity or weakness. Why is agreement impossible? Because one would be redundant or subjugated.
Use the commentary to clarify.
5
Conclude the proof
Since both consequent options are impossible, the antecedent (existence of two gods) must also be impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ الكلام في هذا الفصل مُرتّب على ثلاثة مطالب : الأول : إقامة البرهان على وحدة الذاتِ ؛ بمعنى : نفي تركيبها وعدم انقسامها .
الثاني : نفي نظير له تعالى أو قسيم في الألوهية ، وفي انفراده تعالى بإيجادِ جميع الكائناتِ ذواتاً كانَتْ أو أفعالاً إسنادِ التأثيرِ لغيرِهِ في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ : معناه وعدم
الثالثُ : وَحدتُه تعالى ؛ بمعنى : مخالفته لجميع الحوادثِ ، فلا مثل له منها ، كما أنَّهُ لا ضدَّ له فيها
أما المطلب الأوّلُ : فقد سبق الكلامُ عليهِ عندَ ذكر تنزُّهِهِ تعالى عن الجرمية والتركيب ، فانظره هنالِكَ (۱)
وأمَّا برهانُ المطلب الثاني : فهو الذي تعرض له هنا ، فنقولُ : الدليل على نفْي شريك لهُ تعالى في أُلوهِيَّتِهِ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ مَعَهُ إِلهُ آخِرُ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يختلفا في الإرادة على حكم التضاد أو يتفقا ، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّم مثله أما الملازمةُ : فدليلُها ما سبقَ مِنْ وجوب عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله : وقدرته وسائرِ صفاتِهِ المتعلّقة ، فلو كانَ ثَمَّ إلهانِ لَوجبَ تعلُّقُ إرادةِ كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ بكلّ ممكن ، ومهما تعلّق بالفعل إرادتان لم يخلُ مِنَ الاتفاق عليه أو التباين
وأما بطلان التالي : فببطلان طرفيهِ ؛ وهما الاختلافُ والاتفاق
(۱) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ )
Know that the discussion in this chapter is arranged into three demands: the first: establishing the proof of the unity of the essence, meaning the negation of its composition and its indivisibility. The second: the negation of a peer or counterpart for Him, exalted, in divinity, and in His, exalted, being unique in bringing into existence all beings, whether substances or actions, and the non-attribution of effect to other than Him in anything among possible things: its meaning and [the negation of]... The third: His, exalted, unity, meaning His dissimilarity to all originated things, so there is no likeness to Him from them, just as He has no opposite in them. As for the first demand, the discussion on it has already preceded when mentioning His, exalted, transcendence from corporeality and composition, so look there. (1) As for the proof of the second demand, it is what is presented here. So we say: The evidence for the negation of a partner for Him, exalted, in His divinity is that if there were another god with Him, it would be free from [two possibilities]: either they would differ in will on the basis of contradiction, or they would agree. The consequent, in its two divisions, is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the entailment, its evidence is what has preceded regarding the necessity of the universal relation of the will of God and His power and the rest of His related attributes. For if there were two gods, then the will of each one of them and His power must relate to every possible thing. And whenever two wills relate to an act, they are not free from agreement upon it or divergence. And as for the invalidity of the consequent, it is by the invalidity of its two sides, which are divergence and agreement. (1) It has preceded (p. 248).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a structured presentation of three demands (maṭālib) concerning tawhid (divine oneness). The author first lists them: (1) proof of the unity of the divine essence, i.e., that it is not composed or divisible; (2) negation of any peer or counterpart in divinity, meaning God alone creates and nothing else has real causal influence; (3) God's unity in the sense of being unlike originated things—no likeness, no opposite. The author then notes that the first demand was already covered earlier in the book (at the discussion of transcendence from corporeality). The focus here is the second demand's proof. The proof is a standard kalam argument: if there were a second god, they must either differ or agree in will. Both are impossible: if they differ, one would be thwarted, implying incapacity; if they agree, one is unnecessary or subjugated. The impossibility of both outcomes proves that a second god cannot exist. The author also provides the logical connection (mulāzama): each god's will and power must relate to every possible, so if two wills relate to the same act, they can only agree or differ; both are impossible for a necessary being. Thus, the proof is complete.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The proof demands that if two gods existed, they would either conflict or coincide, both impossible. Both Wills Ineffective: Three Impossibilities
Sanusi enumerates three impossibilities when both gods' wills are ineffective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two gods cannot both have ineffective wills, and the three irrational consequences that follow.

Core Idea
If two gods' wills are both ineffective, they are impotent.

Sanusi continues his proof against two gods by examining the scenario where both gods' wills are ineffective. He argues that this leads to three absurdities: both gods being powerless, the subject being empty of the two contradictories, and either the act existing and not existing by both or prevention without a preventer. This lesson unpacks those three impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part of Sanusi's argument, focusing on the case where both wills are ineffective.
Notice the three impossibilities he lists.
2
Understand the premise
Sanusi assumes two gods differ in their will for the same action. Grasp why effective coexistence of both wills leads to contradiction.
This sets up the need for at least one will to be ineffective.
3
Identify the three impossibilities
List the three impossibilities Sanusi enumerates when both wills are ineffective: (1) both gods impotent, (2) subject empty of both contradictories, (3) either existence and non-existence by both or prevention without preventer.
These are all absurd.
4
Reflect on the logical flow
Trace how Sanusi deduces these impossibilities step by step. Notice the use of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فوجه بطلان الطرفِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو الاختلافُ - أن نقول : لوِ في الفعل ؛ بأنْ يريد أحدهما وجودَ الجوهر ويريد الآخرُ عدمه ، أو يريد أحدهما حركتهُ والآخرُ تسكينَهُ.. لَلزِمَ عجزهما أو عجزُ أحدهما ، معَ زيادة مستحيلاتٍ سنذكرها ؛ وذلك لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً مستحيل ؛ لما يؤدّي إليهِ مِن اجتماع النقيضين أو ما في حكمهما فيكون الجوهرُ في الزمانِ الواحدِ موجوداً معدوماً ، أو متحركاً ساكناً ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ، فإذاً لا بدَّ مِنْ تعطّل النفوذ لإحدى الإرادتين أو لكليهما، فإن تعطَّلَنا معاً لزم عجز الإلهين بتعذّر الفعل مِنْ كلّ واحدٍ منهما ويلزم أيضاً عليه خلو المحل عن النقيضين ، وأيضاً فلا مانع مِنْ نفوذ إرادة كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ .. إلا نفوذ إرادة الآخرِ وقدرته ، فإذا لم تنفذ الإرادتانِ لزم وجود الفعل بهما وعدم وجودِهِ بهما إِنْ ثبت المانع ، أو حصول المنعِ مِنْ غيرِ مانعِ إِنْ لم يثبتِ المانع . فهذهِ ثلاثة أوجه مِنَ الاستحالاتِ ، كلُّها تلزم على تقديرِ تعطيل الإرادتين وأَمَّا إِنْ كانَتْ إرادة أحدهما خاصَّةً هي المتعلقة : فيستحيلُ مِنْ أوجه : أحدها : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ عدمُ عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله وقدرته ، وقد سبق أنَّ ذلك مستحيل ، وإذا استحال لم يمكن أن يكونَ أحد الإلهين أقدرَ مِنَ الآخر ثانيها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ مَنْ لم تنفذ إرادته مع كونه إلها ! والعجز على الإله محال لما سيأتي ثالثها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ الإلهِ الذي نفذَتْ إرادتُهُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُما مثلان ، فيجب لأحدهما ما وجبَ للآخرِ رابعها : الترجيحُ لأحد المثلين على مثلِهِ بصفةٍ مِنْ غيرِ مُرجح فإِنْ فُرِضَ المرجّحُ لزمَ حدوثهما ، ونقلنا الكلامَ إلى الثالث ، ولزمَ التسلسل
Then the aspect of the invalidity of the first side—which is difference—is that we say: if [they differ] in the act, such that one wills the existence of the atom and the other wills its non-existence, or one wills its motion and the other its rest, then their impotence or the impotence of one of them would be necessary, along with additional impossibilities we will mention. That is because the concurrent effectiveness of both wills is impossible due to what it would entail of the conjunction of two contradictories or what is in their ruling; thus the atom would be, in one time, existent and non-existent, or moving and still, and that is inconceivable. Therefore, the ineffectiveness of one of the two wills or of both must occur. If both are ineffective, the impotence of the two gods follows from the impossibility of action from each of them. Also necessary upon this is the vacuity of the subject from the two contradictories. Also, there is no preventer of the effectiveness of the will and power of each except the effectiveness of the will and power of the other. So if the two wills are not effective, then either the existence of the act by both and its non-existence by both is necessary if the preventer is established, or the occurrence of prevention without a preventer if the preventer is not established. These are three aspects of impossibilities, all necessary on the assumption of the ineffectiveness of the two wills. And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that is effective, then it is impossible from several aspects: First: that it entails the lack of universality of the attachment of the will and power of the god, and it has preceded that this is impossible. And if it is impossible, then it cannot be that one of the two gods is more powerful than the other. Second: that it entails the impotence of the one whose will is not effective despite being a god, and impotence upon a god is impossible as will come. Third: that it entails the impotence of the god whose will is effective as well, because they are equals, so what is necessary for one is necessary for the other. Fourth: the preponderance of one of the two equals over its equal by a quality without a preponderant, and if the preponderant is assumed, their temporality is necessary, and we would transfer the discussion to a third, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a proof by contradiction against the existence of two gods. Sanusi first considers what would happen if two gods differed in their will regarding a single act (e.g., one wants a substance to exist, the other wants it not to exist). He argues that if both wills were effective, contradictory states would coexist (e.g., the substance would be both existent and non-existent at the same time), which is absurd. Therefore, at least one will must be ineffective. If both are ineffective, both gods are impotent, which is impossible for a god. If only one is ineffective, then that god is impotent, and the other also becomes impotent because they are equals. Additionally, preferring one equal over another without a preponderant leads to infinite regress. Thus, difference between gods is impossible.

Key Terms
اختلاف
الإلهين
الإرادة
القدرة
عجز
نفوذ
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for two gods to both have omnipotence even if they disagree.
Correction: Sanusi shows that disagreement leads to either both being impotent or one impotent, contradicting omnipotence.

Checkpoint
What is the first impossibility Sanusi mentions when both wills are ineffective?
A) Both gods become powerless.
B) One god overpowers the other.
C) The atom becomes eternal.
D) The universe ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
Two gods with differing wills cannot both be omnipotent; if both wills fail, three absurdities follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is one and alone in His power, does your daily life reflect that trust, or do you sometimes act as if other forces (people, systems, fate) have independent power alongside Him? What would change if you fully internalized this lesson's conclusion that only one omnipotent will can exist?
?
When you face a situation where your will conflicts with another's, do you instinctively assume that one of you must be right and the other wrong, as the world would be absurd otherwise? How does this lesson's logic about two gods inform your understanding of conflict resolution in human relationships?
?
The argument shows that two opposite wills cannot both be effective. Where in your own life are you trying to hold onto contradictory commitments—like desiring both worldly success and spiritual detachment—expecting both to succeed? What would it mean to let one go and align your will with God's?

Only One Will Effective: Four Impossibilities
Sanusi gives four reasons why only one effective will also impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the case where only one god's will is effective also leads to impossibility, through four specific arguments.

Core Idea
If one god's will alone is effective, that god is still not truly omnipotent.

After showing that both wills being ineffective is impossible, Sanusi turns to the alternative: one will is effective and the other is not. He argues that this too leads to absurdities. In this lesson, we explore the four impossibilities he lists, including the lack of universal will, the impotence of the ineffective god, the consequent impotence of the effective god due to equality, and the problem of preponderance without a preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that is effective...' to the end.
This section covers four aspects.
2
Understand the first impossibility
If only one will is effective, that god's will is not universal (does not apply to all things), contradicting omnipotence.
Sanusi references a prior proof that God's will is universal.
3
Analyze the second and third impossibilities
The god with ineffective will is impotent; but since the two are equals, the effective god is also impotent by parity.
Equality (مثلان) is key.
4
Examine the fourth impossibility
Preferring one equal over another without a preponderant leads to infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is a classic kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the aspect of the invalidity of the first side—which is difference—is that we say: if [they differ] in the act, such that one wills the existence of the atom and the other wills its non-existence, or one wills its motion and the other its rest, then their impotence or the impotence of one of them would be necessary, along with additional impossibilities we will mention. That is because the concurrent effectiveness of both wills is impossible due to what it would entail of the conjunction of two contradictories or what is in their ruling; thus the atom would be, in one time, existent and non-existent, or moving and still, and that is inconceivable. Therefore, the ineffectiveness of one of the two wills or of both must occur. If both are ineffective, the impotence of the two gods follows from the impossibility of action from each of them. Also necessary upon this is the vacuity of the subject from the two contradictories. Also, there is no preventer of the effectiveness of the will and power of each except the effectiveness of the will and power of the other. So if the two wills are not effective, then either the existence of the act by both and its non-existence by both is necessary if the preventer is established, or the occurrence of prevention without a preventer if the preventer is not established. These are three aspects of impossibilities, all necessary on the assumption of the ineffectiveness of the two wills. And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that is effective, then it is impossible from several aspects: First: that it entails the lack of universality of the attachment of the will and power of the god, and it has preceded that this is impossible. And if it is impossible, then it cannot be that one of the two gods is more powerful than the other. Second: that it entails the impotence of the one whose will is not effective despite being a god, and impotence upon a god is impossible as will come. Third: that it entails the impotence of the god whose will is effective as well, because they are equals, so what is necessary for one is necessary for the other. Fourth: the preponderance of one of the two equals over its equal by a quality without a preponderant, and if the preponderant is assumed, their temporality is necessary, and we would transfer the discussion to a third, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a proof by contradiction against the existence of two gods. Sanusi first considers what would happen if two gods differed in their will regarding a single act (e.g., one wants a substance to exist, the other wants it not to exist). He argues that if both wills were effective, contradictory states would coexist (e.g., the substance would be both existent and non-existent at the same time), which is absurd. Therefore, at least one will must be ineffective. If both are ineffective, both gods are impotent, which is impossible for a god. If only one is ineffective, then that god is impotent, and the other also becomes impotent because they are equals. Additionally, preferring one equal over another without a preponderant leads to infinite regress. Thus, difference between gods is impossible.

Key Terms
تعلق
عموم
عجز
مثلان
الترجيح
المرجح
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for one god to be superior to another in power.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if one god's will is more effective, the other is impotent and equality entails both impotent.

Checkpoint
What is the second impossibility when only one will is effective?
A) The effective will lacks universality.
B) The god with ineffective will is impotent.
C) The effective god becomes impotent.
D) Preponderance without preponderant.

Key Takeaway
Even if one god's will alone is effective, it leads to contradictions including impotence and irrational preponderance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what specific area of your life are you acting as if there is a will more effective than Allah's? What concrete action can you take today to affirm His absolute sovereignty over that area?
?
When your plans clash with Allah's decree, do you inwardly rebel or accept your own impotence? How can the truth that only Allah's will is effective change your response the next time you face disappointment?
?
If you genuinely believed that every event—including your failures and trials—proceeds from a single, perfect will, how would your anxiety or despair be transformed? Will you trust this truth in your next difficulty?

Impossibility of Agreement Between Two Gods
Sanusi argues that agreement between two gods leads to coercion and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that necessary agreement between two gods would imply coercion, contradicting divine freedom.

Core Idea
Necessary agreement between two gods would make them coerced, not free — impossible for true divinity.

Sanusi continues the refutation of multiple gods by examining whether they could always agree. He argues that if their agreement is necessary, then each is coerced and not free, which contradicts the essence of God as the Freely Acting Creator. This passage lays out the logical consequences of such a scenario.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of agreement
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi divides agreement into obligatory (واجب) and possible (جائز).
This division is the foundation of his argument.
2
Understand the argument for obligatory agreement
Focus on the consequences of obligatory agreement: each god would be coerced and not free. Consider the two subcases: both unable to oppose, or one able and the other not.
Coercion follows in both subcases.
3
Reflect on the Quranic citation and further implications
Note the citation of Quran 28:68 to emphasize divine freedom. Then consider the additional points: coercion of one implies coercion of the other, the need for a preponderant, and the conversion of possible to impossible.
These reinforce the absurdity of obligatory agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلانُ الطرف الثاني مِنَ التالي ـ وهو الاتفاقُ ـ فمِنْ أوجه : وذلكَ أنَّ الاتفاق : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ واجباً أو جائزاً ، ويلزم في الاتفاقِ الواجب أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما مقهوراً غير مختارِ إنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما لا يَقدِرُ على مخالفة الآخرِ ، وإنْ كانَ أَحدهما يَقدِرُ عليها دونَ الآخرِ .. لزمَ قهرُ الذي لا يَقدِرُ عليها ، ونفي كونه مختاراً ؛ لأنَّ المختار هو الذي يتأتّى منهُ الفعلُ والتركُ ، فإذا كانَ اتفاقهما معاً ، أو أحدهما واجباً .. لم يتأتَ مِنَ المجبورِ منهما ترْكُ ما اختارَهُ الآخرُ ، كيف وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختارُ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] ؟ ! وأيضاً : يلزمُ مِنْ قهْرِ أحدهما قهْرُ الآخرِ ؛ لأنَّه مثله ، ويلزمُ الافتقار إلى المرجّح في تخصيص أحدِ المثلين بما لم يثبت لمثلِهِ ويلزم أيضاً في الاتفاقِ الواجب : انقلاب الممكن مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّ كل واحدٍ منهما إذا نظرنا إليهِ منفرداً أمكن أن يُوجِدَ كلاً مِنَ الحركة
And as for the invalidity of the second side of the consequent—which is agreement—it is from several aspects: That is because agreement: either it is obligatory or possible. In obligatory agreement, it is necessary that each of them is coerced, not free, if each of them is unable to oppose the other. And if one is able to oppose without the other, then coercion of the one who is unable follows, and denial of his being free; because the free one is he from whom both action and leaving are possible. So if their agreement together or that of one is obligatory, then leaving what the other chose is not possible for the coerced one from among them. How, and 'your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Quran 28:68)? Also, from the coercion of one follows the coercion of the other because he is like him, and need for a preponderant is required in specifying one of the equals with what is not established for its like. Also, in obligatory agreement is required the conversion of the possible into impossible; because each of them, when we look at him individually, it is possible for him to bring into existence the whole motion...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of two gods by examining the possibility that they always agree. Sanusi divides agreement into two types: necessary (obligatory) and possible. If their agreement is necessary, then each is coerced and not free (since they cannot oppose each other). If one can oppose but the other cannot, the one who cannot is coerced and not free. He cites Quran 28:68 to emphasize that God creates and chooses freely. Furthermore, coercion of one implies coercion of the other because they are equals, leading to the need for an external preponderant to specify one equal over the other, which would entail infinite regress or temporal origination. He also points out that necessary agreement turns what is possible (independent action) into impossible, which is absurd. The argument is incomplete in this window but lays out key inconsistencies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument proves that God is coerced.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that if there were two gods with necessary agreement, they would be coerced, which is impossible for God. Thus, multiple gods cannot exist.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Necessary agreement between two gods would require them to be coerced, not free, which contradicts the nature of God and is therefore impossible.

What specific step will you take to dethrone a false 'god' in your life this week?
?

How does recognizing that Allah alone is perfectly free and uncoerced change the way you view your struggles?
